Reid Hoffman On The Case For AI Optimism

Prof G Markets(49.2K) ·71:41 ·23.8K views · 2026-05-01
Reid Hoffman (Co-founder, LinkedIn) Scott Galloway (Host, Prof G Markets) Ed Elson (Host, Prof G Markets)
Reid Hoffman argues that AI represents a "Cognitive Industrial Revolution" where success is measured by technological frontier-building rather than short-term financials; while labor displacement in scripted roles is inevitable, the focus must shift ...
TL;DR Reid Hoffman argues that AI represents a "Cognitive Industrial Revolution" where success is measured by technological frontier-building rather than short-term financials; while labor displacement in scripted roles is inevitable, the focus must shift to using AI to...

TL;DR

Reid Hoffman argues that AI represents a "Cognitive Industrial Revolution" where success is measured by technological frontier-building rather than short-term financials; while labor displacement in scripted roles is inevitable, the focus must shift to using AI to democratize essential services like healthcare and education.

The State of OpenAI and the Path to IPO

  • Investment Philosophy: Hoffman, an early investor in OpenAI and a Microsoft board member, dismissed concerns regarding OpenAI missing revenue and user targets.
      • Prioritizing Frontier Models: He argues that the quality of technology (e.g., the 5.5 release) is the leading indicator for future growth, regardless of current quarter-over-quarter reliability.
      • Public Market Scrutiny: Hoffman compares AI companies to early Amazon or Tesla—entities that trade on a "foundational promise" of the future rather than traditional P/E ratios.
  • Unit Economics: The industry is currently in a race to an "asymptote" where the exponential cost of training models eventually meets the physical limits of power (gigawatts) and capital.
      • Inference vs. Training: While training costs are skyrocketing, the economics for "inference" (running the models for users) are already becoming viable and attractive.

The Global Competitive Landscape

  • Tiered Rankings: Hoffman identifies a distinct hierarchy among the major players:
      • Tier 1 (The Leaders): OpenAI and Anthropic currently hold the strongest positions in terms of frontier capabilities.
      • Tier 2 (Big Tech): Google (Gemini) is the primary challenger, followed by Meta and Microsoft’s internal efforts.
      • The "Glengarry Glen Ross" Market: In specialized fields like coding, Hoffman notes a "winner-takes-most" dynamic. Currently, Claude Code (Anthropic) leads in interaction, while OpenAI Codex leads in long-form reasoning.
  • The China Factor: Chinese models like ByteDance’s multimodal video tools and the Qwen open-source model are highly credible but often rely on "distillation" (learning from Western frontier models). Hoffman believes future distillation will become significantly harder.
  • Apple's Lag: Hoffman noted that Apple appears behind the curve, remarking that "they still don't seem to realize that Siri is like 20 years old in tech terms."

AI Risks: Cybersecurity and Labor

  • Mythos and the "Crime Scene" Computer: Hoffman addressed Anthropic's warnings about "Mythos" (their latest agentic capability).
      • Automated Penetration Testing: AI allows for an "infinite" number of virtual cybersecurity engineers. This fundamentally shifts the landscape by allowing rogue actors to probe billions of lines of legacy code that were previously too expensive to attack.
  • The Cognitive Industrial Revolution: Hoffman admits the transition will be painful, specifically for "scripted" labor.
      • High-Risk Sectors: Customer service jobs are expected to "go completely away," and sales roles will undergo massive structural shifts.
      • Historical Parallel: Much like the Industrial Revolution eliminated "horse-cleanup jobs" but created the automotive industry, AI will create new, currently unpredictable roles.
      • Transition Strategy: Hoffman advocates for deploying AI *for* the transition—using it to help displaced workers find new roles and acquire new skills.

Social Responsibility and the "80% Solution"

  • Democratizing Expertise: Hoffman suggests that governments should negotiate deals with AI labs to provide "free-at-point-of-use" AI assistants on every smartphone.
      • Essential Services: These would include a medical assistant, a legal assistant, and a tutor for every citizen to offset the economic "melee" of the transition.
  • Wealth Inequality vs. Prosperity: Hoffman is less concerned with "inequality" as a metric and more focused on whether the "bulk of 80% of society" sees their circumstances improve.
      • Progressive Taxation: While he supports progressive taxation to fix social dysfunctions, he argues it is not a sufficient solution on its own without fundamental economic renovation.

Legal Battles and Market Speculation

  • Elon Musk vs. OpenAI: Hoffman characterized Musk’s lawsuit against OpenAI as "legal harassment" and "factually incorrect." He claims Musk attempted to take 80% ownership of the company and left when the board refused, only to sue later when the organization succeeded.
  • SpaceX IPO Rumors: Hoffman expressed skepticism regarding a potential SpaceX IPO, suggesting that some investors may be buying in based on "Bitcoin-style" momentum rather than sane investing behavior. He noted rumors that Musk might eventually attempt to merge SpaceX with Tesla to bolster market cap.

***

Notable Quotes

"I tend to think a little bit on the coding side... it’s a Glengarry Glen Ross market: first prize Cadillac, second prize steak knives, third prize you're fired." — Reid Hoffman
"One of the things that's been a bit of a social problem over the last few decades has been that so much of the growth has been contained only to the private markets... I think that's one of the benefits of having some of these AI companies go public." — Reid Hoffman
"The Industrial Age was really painful in terms of its transition... I think the same thing is true of the Cognitive Industrial Revolution with AI, but the transition is really painful. How do we make the transition better? The simplest thing is: deploy AI for it." — Reid Hoffman

He has also created, advised, and invested in some of the largest and most successful technology companies in the world. Few individuals sit closer to what might turn out to be the most transformative technology of our time. And so we want to find out what is actually happening on the ground. Here is our conversation with Reid Hoffman, co-founder of LinkedIn, Inflection, and Manas AI, partner at Greylock, and author of "Superagency: What Could Possibly Go Right with Our AI Future". Reid, great to have you on the show. Thank you for joining us. I want to start with some specific news that we saw this week that is making investors a little bit anxious, and that is this OpenAI news, specifically that they missed their revenue target last year. They also missed their user target, and they're obviously set to go public soon. It should be one of the most important IPOs ever, and suddenly investors are very anxious about this company. They are anxious that they're actually not growing in the way that they had expected. And you are in an interesting position because one, you're on the board of Microsoft, and Microsoft is obviously one of the largest shareholders in OpenAI, one of the earliest investors, and also you are an early investor in OpenAI through your VC firm Greylock. So let's just start with this. What do you make of this news, and should investors be worried about OpenAI? Well, ultimately, as an investor, I'm not worried. I mean, part of it is the company had very aggressive targets, and so when you have aggressive targets and you kind of go in a little below them, that's actually not the kind of thing that I worry about that much. And classically, the reason why public companies—because you know, most public investors tend to want reliability quarter to quarter—you tend to do your best to be on target in terms of what they're saying because there's this kind of reliability thing. Whereas I as a private investor tend to be the "just establish the really, really strong basis". And so you know, for me, the thing I wasn't tracking so much was last year's revenue or last year's user count. Those are great, making progress, obviously we'll need to grow more too, but a lot more. But the real question that I was like very happy to see was the 5.5 release, because I think the thing that I'm most tracking is whether OpenAI is continuing to deliver some of the world's best technology, frontier models and so forth. And you know, that's I think the precursor to everything else, and that from every source I've seen and multiple different benchmarks and multiple different engagements has been the kind of new world leader in quality of model. So that is more of what I pay attention to because it's—you know, with investing, it's downstream effects. It's: what does next year look like? That is the most relevant question. Does that make you concerned? It's an interesting point. Like early-stage investors, venture capitalists, they're more interested in the technology, they're more interested in what's going to happen 10, 15, 20 years down the line. Public markets investors are more interested in what's happening right now. I mean, we're literally reading quarterly earnings, we look at what happened last year, we look at what happened in the previous quarter. It's much more of a sort of backward-looking practice, which seems to be like it might be a big problem for OpenAI if they are to go public at the end of this year because suddenly they will be scrutinized for their financials. And so I wonder if that concerns you that suddenly the company is leaving an ecosystem of people and investors that are okay with financials that might not make sense right now, but then suddenly they're going and pitching the company to a group of investors who care a lot about that stuff. Are you worried that maybe they will get punished or that they will have a hard time in the public markets when that time comes? Well, if they can't establish themselves, there is a small set of companies—and it isn't just Tesla, which is probably the extreme example of this, which is kind of betting on the future—but Amazon for a significant number of years, and others that say, "Hey, I've got a foundational promise of where I'm going" where investors go, "We believe in that future, and so we're buying in that future." And the fact that the current P/E comparables and other kinds of things don't make sense on a quarter-by-quarter basis, even factoring in an interesting CAGR or anything else. I think it'll be for all of the AI companies that are kind of prepping and considering going public, it'll be important to establish that basis because I think the first year or two will look a lot like early dot-com companies, some of which were complete flameouts and some of which were enduring institutions, Amazon being an example. What do you make of the unit economics right now? Because this is something I've been thinking about when we think about these companies like 10 years down the line where currently the unit economics don't really make sense. Like the cost to build these models is so incredibly high, and that is why OpenAI and Anthropic, any of the sort of foundation labs, are burning a lot of money right now. It almost seems as if the only way that this works out from the perspective of the business model is for these companies to become like essentially utility companies, like almost have these monopolies over compute. And Sam Altman has said this; he has said that eventually he could see OpenAI becoming something like a utility company down the line, which to me is quite an interesting perspective. Because it's quite different from what we saw in the dot-com boom where we've had, you know, a handful of companies—we might call it an oligopoly—but a handful of companies operating in the same space. I mean, where do you see the business model trending further down the line? Obviously, you know, as you know, you have to distinguish between the training costs and the inference costs. And one of the things when you look at these numbers is that the inference costs are actually in fact pretty good economics for inference, but obviously you have an exponentiating training category. And so the question is, as you're doing this exponentiating training—and I do think that at some point the training costs, even though there's a goal to get as strong as you can there because then your quality of model kind of dominates through all the different inference units. Essentially, at some level of exponential, that starts asymptoting just because it doesn't make sense within, you know, how many gigawatts do we have? What kind of economic cost do we get there? And I think a little bit of the race is a race to that asymptote. Now, where exactly that asymptote is, I think is somewhat governed by availability of capital from investors believing, and then the delivery of revenue. Now, part of the delivery of revenue is not just the current services, where you say, "Okay, you know, OpenAI is dominating the chatbot and kind of what the consumer interaction with that service is." Anthropic is doing the coding side, followed by OpenAI on the coding side in terms of how the API and coding works. But like, this has got to be just the beginning. Part of how you're looking at these AI models is not just the provision of tokens, which obviously is interesting across a number of things, but what is the output that comes from? Now that software engineering can be much more broadly spread in a whole bunch of areas which otherwise wouldn't have been able to afford software engineering, it can now do them. That can affect the productivity of their areas. What happens there? What happens in services firms when a bunch of different services—take legal or accounting or anything else—can be done with intensive amplification of this? It's not just a question of, you know, paying seats in a software basis, but what is the delivery in terms of the margins of what the delivery of the service is? And where all those economics play out is I think very early in TBD. And you'd say, "Well, should we wait to go public for those?" I actually think one of the things when you—you two know this better than I do—but I think one of the things that's been a bit of a kind of call it a social problem over the last few decades has been that so much of the growth has been contained only to the private markets because of like, let's delay going public until it's extremely stable. Which means that a lot of folks who cannot participate in the private markets only get exposure to the public markets. And I think that's one of the benefits of having some of these AI companies. But I think it's early relative to what is the business model going to be and then what is that revenue going to be. And now utility, I think in terms of the fact that any task that you do with language or information, I think will have AI participation at some level of depth, whether it's complete automation, whether it's augmentation, whether it's assistance in various ways. And so the spread of that is I think a good measure on utility. I think one of the things that's good for society is the fact that we have multiple providers of these frontier models, so they're competing with each other in terms of pricing, in terms of availability to entrepreneurs building other kinds of different services, in terms of being able to—you know, like the best governance mechanism is, you know, relative to the health of society for the cost of things. Oligopoly, monopoly, etc., is to have competition which then brings the price down, and I think that's something that we are also seeing. So the utility question around kind of around the—like should it run like utility?—you know, I don't know how well utilities are thought of in the UK. In the US, utilities are kind of a disaster. So you don't want it run like that. So I'd love to just get your—I mean granted you might have a bias because you're on the board of Microsoft, but and you're an investor in several AI platforms or LLMs—can you give us a lay of the land in terms of the competitive set? You know, obviously OpenAI, Anthropic, Google with Gemini, and then the Chinese models and I'm sure there's a bunch of others, but give us what you think is the competitive landscape right now. So I think—and this is to some degree good news for startups and productivity startups—I think the strongest positions are OpenAI and Anthropic. I think in the traditional big companies, Gemini is next, and I think will have a bunch of different efforts from Meta, Microsoft on its own, obviously used a lot of OpenAI right now, and Amazon. Who knows where Apple will play out in all of this stuff since, you know, they still don't seem to realize that Siri is like 20 years old kind of in tech terms. Now for the Chinese models, the interesting question will be like—there's a lot of good open-source models that pretty clearly have some roots in distillation from kind of the major Western frontier models. And I think part of what we're going into is an area where that distillation will get a lot harder. Now, that being said, the Chinese are building up compute, building up chips, have extraordinary amounts of talent, hardworking, great tech companies. Matter of fact, the SeaDance multimodal video model is I think amongst the best in the world. So I think it's already, you know, you already have stuff that's kind of playing there. But like and then you kind of have a jagged edge. Like you go, "Okay, let's go coding-specific." So most developers will say Claude Code. Part of the reason is because Claude Code has got the best model, I think the best interaction thing for iterating through kind of the amplification of a software engineer. But then the in-depth engineers that I know who play with them go OpenAI Codex because it's much more useful at like call it 20, 30, 50, 90 minute reasoning long tasks that play to harder engineering, and so they prefer that versus Claude Code. And right now, at the moment, that's it when you get to like people who have exposure to it. Now some people then go, "Okay, I can't afford either of those, so I'll use Qwen," you know, the the Chinese open-source model, which is quite credible, but when you think about the fact that when you're looking for coding, just as one instance, you're actually looking for something that's quite good and quite reliable that doesn't introduce bugs. Obviously, we have Mythos coming with questions around cybersecurity and what does this all mean there, and that matters too. So the I tend to think a little bit on the coding side, for your particular coding problem, it's a little bit like what we call in blitzscaling a Glengarry Glen Ross market, which is, you know, first prize Cadillac, second prize steak knives, third prize you're fired. So roughly. But then it's different across, you know, like images—the OpenAI image generator is I think now like—like I've been wanting to create graphic novels for eight years with AI, and I now think I can. So that's interesting. Anyway, we could spend the entire time on the different capabilities of these. So it's a good segue into talking about Mythos. So Anthropic described Mythos; he said that it turns every computer into a crime scene. And I'm trying to distinguish between what is responsible warnings about their own product and catastrophizing as a means of fundraising. Because everyone's talking about Mythos right now and there's sort of this—and it's prevalent I think across a lot of AI—that my technology is so amazing that it's going to reshape the world both good and bad. Please invest in my Series D at 100 times revenues. How much of this is responsible warnings from, you know, Dr. Frankenstein that Frank could be dangerous, and how much of it is just quite frankly is just fundraising? One, I think the Anthropic people are very earnest, principled, and honest. So I think there's a fundamental basis which is completely honest. And here's a simple way of making that case, which is if you said the minimum that Mythos is is an ability to have an infinite number of quality cybersecurity engineers who are penetration testers—and it's not infinite obviously because there's a compute cost for running them, but you could take a thousand of them and run them in a direction—you end up even if it's just that, you end up with a re-changing of the cybersecurity landscape. Because we have billions of lines of code that essentially haven't been touched, and partially because there hasn't been either an economic model for the number of cybercriminals or the number of people employed by rogue states to go after any other than a certain set of systems. But it broadens the range by the ability to just kind of spin up many new penetration testing engineers with a kind of an AI mechanism. And that's the minimum that I think you could look at the discussion around Mythos and its cybersecurity issues. I think that it's actually somewhat better than that, and I don't mean that that's amazing, what I just said. But I don't know yet how much more. Right? Like is it "Oh my gosh, you put a top 10% cybersecurity engineer and put them against, you know, a couple of Mythos agents, and do they outpace that person?" It's unclear to me. Does it think in ways that we haven't thought of before and generate new patterns? That's unclear to me. But you don't need those to have this be a massive kind of shift in how we think about cybersecurity, what are the kinds of things we need to do. And so I actually think that they're doing a responsible thing by saying, "There's an important change coming, and we need to get at least the essential systems ready." We'll grant you more than a minute to answer this because it's a difficult—or you know, we're going to need a bigger boat. But Andrew Yang, who was on our podcast a few days ago, said, you know, he's having a moment, right? Because he's sort of in some ways predicted all the fears, the sum of all fears around capital destroying labor. And there's already evidence around youth unemployment increasing, a lack of hiring, layoffs at some of the early adopters. We—I won't use "we"—I have taken the view that we have been to this movie before and some of the catastrophizing around the destruction in the labor market doesn't recognize the new opportunities and the new jobs that'll be created. So I would love to just get your thought and take as whatever time you need to describe what you think the impact on the labor market is going to be of AI. Every job that uses language or information will have an AI component that will range in certain cases of automation, in certain cases much more AI work, and in certain cases kind of AI amplification or augmentation of human labor. And I think that will be true of all of those things. And so almost every industry, because if you think about like even steel manufacture, you still have meetings, financial analysis, capital, legal, strategic planning, and so forth. All that plays into it. So that means that AI will touch everything. Now, and I by the way inherently also agree with you that one of the general problems when you get new technology—I kind of put this in superagency—is you go, "Well, I can see if I take jobs as a fixed number that do not grow and do not change, and I can see that a bunch of them will be gone." "Now the horses that carry the buggies will go away and we'll have cars, and like all the groomspeople will go away and all the horse carrying jobs will go away and all the horse cleanup jobs will go away." I can tell that and be like, "Well, but you didn't predict all the new cars, the new jobs and drivers and blah blah blah in that particular thing and you can predict the negative changes and you can't predict the positive ones." So now the rub in this is there's a couple of places where AI creates kind of multi-industry, very fast, much cheaper things. Like say for example if you said, "Well, you know, like for example one of the areas where I think you'll see a lot of automation is where human beings are following a script." The AI will follow a script much better. So I think customer service jobs go completely away. But by the way, I think like sales jobs transfer a whole bunch too, and what does the new sales universe look like? Both of them are lots of body employers, and so you get some pretty big transitions in terms of of how this plays. And that's the thing is I actually think the transition points will be, like more likely than not, challenging to navigate. Now part of the reason like I still tend to be—we just should we should accelerate as much as possible and use it—even though we're going to have these transition difficulties, but the thing we should be doing is saying, "Well with AI, how do we help in these transition difficulties?" Namely, how do we say, "Oh, if you're no longer have a customer service job..." Which they haven't really been decreasing yet and so therefore a little bit of the college people not being hired and so forth. It's much easier for companies to say, "We're doing layoffs because of AI," than "We mishired in the pandemic, there's global instability, we're not investing in the future at the moment because of global instability, etc.," so "No, no, we're strong because we're using AI." So there's a lot of discourse on that. But I actually think that the customer service jobs will be the as one of the kind of canaries in the coal mine. We have to have customer service, and all of a sudden the jobs start going down because of Sierra or Parloa or whatever. Then I think that you'll begin to see some of that. Now I think you'll see it; it's just a question of speed, time, how, etc. And then we get to the transformation transitions, and part of the transition is let's deploy AI for that. Let's deploy AI for: "Okay, I no longer have a customer service job, how do I find what other jobs could it work with me?" What other jobs could I do? What are the things that you could help me learn? What are the things you could help me do? What are the jobs you can help me find? And when I think about this from a society point of view, because these kinds of workforce transformations—you know, the industrial age was really painful in terms of its transformation—we don't have any of the wonderful things we have without the Industrial Revolution. And I think the same thing is true of the Cognitive Industrial Revolution with AI, but the transition is really painful. How do we make the transition better? And I think the simplest thing is deploy AI for it. You'd have to be pretty blind not to be concerned about the popularity problem. And you know it's manifest in such things also as weird like "We should ban data centers." And you're like, "Okay, you want the data centers built in Canada or somewhere else as opposed to here?" Most of the arguments against data centers are pretty spurious. I think the key question around the local economy is say, "Hey, make it a good enough trade for us to bring it here, like bring in some economic prosperity. Sure, the construction jobs and the running jobs aren't a lot of jobs, but maybe there's some other things you can bring in." "Like maybe you could bring in a plus 10% of your energy usage, you add 10% that helps us in a structural way." I mean there's all kinds of different ways you can do that. Now that being said, you know, for example, the UK didn't invent the Industrial Revolution but embraced it strongly and had centuries of global prosperity from it. And so I do think the transition is challenging, but I think the embrace is valuable. And I think that the question around, "What should we do?" is like it's important from an economic—it's like it is the driving force of what the future economies are, and it'll only work for future generations if we embrace the right economic things. But I think the key thing is to try to figure out what's the way we navigate better. Like I think anyone who says, "Oh we navigated the Industrial Revolution perfectly," is out of their mind or ahistorical. Look, what's the learnings? What are the things we can do? Now the most often thing that people who are not building within the just call it competitive business world or competitive technology world say is, "We'll just slow everything down." Right? And you're like, "Okay, the problem is competition doesn't slow down." That's actually not the way that that kind of competition works. If you just said, "Hey everyone, wait for me to get my economy in order and then you can start shipping your export things"—I mean, take a look at what's going to be happening within Europe with the spread of BYD cars from Hungary, for example. It's like, "Oh no, no wait for a decade for us to get our auto industry in place." That's not going to happen in terms of how this operates. So you have to be figuring it out how to make the adjustments and transformation at the speed that—here we are in a markets podcast—the markets are bringing about. And that's the thing is to say: give good ideas on the transformation that we can embrace to help make the transformation better. And that's part of the reason why I gestured at the speed and depth of AI also gives us tools to help with the transformation. I don't think it's wrong to produce a goal of wealth. I think every networked system—cities, trade, everything else—creates economic inequality. And economic inequality is part of the engine by which we fuel capitalism and competition and all the rest. So it's like, just to be clear, I think that's a fine thing. I do think the question is how do you bring the bulk of society along. E.g., how do you have benefit that spreads throughout all society? And I'd say like for example is one instance, Sam Altman's gotten a bunch of bad press over the last X months, and yet in the very early days he was funding universal basic income experiments, trying to figure out what kinds of things would work there, and spending money directly himself in order to do that. So I'd say that there are people who actually in fact pay attention to the issue, care about the issue; it's one of the things I don't think Sam gets enough credit for, as just one instance. Now that being said, the question is to say, all right, are we putting in time and energy in order to help society? I think that a number of the different players—definitely Microsoft, Google, Anthropic, OpenAI, and some others—are interested and willing to do that. They're not willing to do it if it's fruitless relative to its engagement and everything else given that they also have an intense competition clock going. So if you wanted to say like one of the things that I've been telling governments for years is: the kind of things you should be asking for is: I'd like to have a medical assistant that runs on every smartphone that can help people, I'd like to have a legal assistant that runs on every smartphone that can help people, I'd like to have a tutor that runs on every smartphone. And essentially make those the equivalent of free as part of the benefit for helping this transition. Like I think you could do that and I think you could very easily cut the deals in which they would build it and so then the government could make sure it's there for everybody and to be getting some benefits in as we sort out the kind of melee of what's going on. But like say for example they just say, "Okay, we'll go build the tutor that helps with all things economic and jobs." Well, okay, we build it, are we going to use it? How is it going to get there? Do we have to spend energy and time on that too? So given that there's this focused competition about where the primary economics are and the establishment of a platform and so forth. So this is kind of an area where I think they are legitimately concerned. Part of the reason why they in their own minds will say—pretty and this is, you know, Scott was gesturing at you know—kind of like the "Oh are you just fundraising by saying 'white-collar bloodbath in X years', you know, buy my Series D." And I don't think that's a great way to try to sell the Series D. I think it's actually like, "Hey, shit's coming, somebody should be helping do something here and we're happy to help, we got our shit that we're working on but we're happy to put some energy into it." And I think that's what we need to be doing, you know. Now unfortunately at the moment, saying "well-run government" seems to be something on kind of on the order of dinosaurs or dodo birds or anything else, and it's difficult to figure out what to do. I'm always happy whenever a government of any Western democracy calls me and says, "I am trying to figure this out, what can I do to help?" Like I'm like, "Okay, here's some ideas, here's things," like you know the medical assistants one that I've been talking about since 2018. Right, because you want to say, "Hey, this AI transformation is challenging." Most people do not have immediate access to medical advice, right? The vast majority of the world does not have that. And it's like "Oh, could I get some that's good?" and that would be easy for governments to set up because it's changing the legal liability and then the mandate for how it's reached, and then having a deal with whatever number of companies you want in order to go into that in terms of what you do, and then all of a sudden you're providing something. I tend to not focus as much on the inequality question as much as on—like if you say the kind of bulk of 80% of society, is their circumstances improving in some substantive way? And I tend to think that if they think, "Hey, I've got some reasonable opportunity playing the game, my circumstances can improve, they might be improving," then I think you get more social stability. And I think it's when a—"I don't know how to feed my children, I think my prospects over the next decade are just categorically worse, etc., etc."—is I think when you hit it. And it's not the usual kind of thing that I tend to say is inequality tends to be more of a political topic because it's like, "Well, the CEO should only be paid 20 times as much as the bottom person." You're like, "Well how do you get to that magic number? Why isn't it two? Why isn't it 5,000?" You know, you let markets sort those things out. And by the way, inequality comes with the economic system that we have built prosperity in: capitalism and competition. Cities generate higher wealth because of network density, trade generates higher wealth, and it always has some disparate curve. It's not like it's evenly spread like peanut butter across the whole system. Now that being said, it is absolutely mandatory for healthy societies to be saying, "Hey, I as a middle-class family am viewing myself as having better prospects as I'm going into it." I might have a rough year or two, I might have some way that I have to work hard or anything else, but that kind of thing is an option. And I think that needs a strong solution. I think that right now, I think people are like, "Look, times are hard." It's part of the reason why I think democracies around the world tend to be saying, "Hey, we want to elect someone who's going to take a wrecking ball to everything because we think maybe that's the only way that things could get better." That almost always leads to things being worse: Cultural Revolution, French Terror, etc., etc. Renovation is the important thing, but I get the frustration and the anger, the uncertainty that gets there. And I think it's beholden on all of us to try to say, "Hey, let's try to renovate these institutions together and let's try to make it work for the bulk of 80%." And whatever it takes to get there. Because, for example, if you said the simple problem is inequality, it's like, "Well, then let's just simply institute a 90% upper bound tax on stuff," and that should just simply solve everything. I don't think that would actually in fact solve everything. Like I think someone did an estimate that if you took one of the super billionaires, Elon or Bezos or whoever, and simply redistributed their economics across the entire US country, it makes a difference—everyone's small savings account gets a little bit bigger—but it doesn't fundamentally change anything there. So you want to be changing the actual economic system of what's happening. Oh just to be clear I'm for progressive taxation. This wasn't an inequality point as being the—like that's the problem of society and what we need to solve. How do we raise as much of the kind of call it 80 plus percent of people as we can? And whatever that took in progressive taxation, that's awesome from my point of view. I just think that progressive taxation itself I don't think is sufficient to the solution, right? Like it's by the way I completely agree. The differential—there's a reason why I think in most of the places where we've had strong growth in societies we've had progressive taxation. The fact that the wealthy can hack it in ways that they're paying less tax than a middle-class person as a percentage is essentially a multifunction of society and that that should be fixed. Right? But that's fixed for a different reason other than pure inequality questions. I was around and basically Elon I think is doing everything possible to throw anything he could possibly invent at OpenAI. Among them was claiming that they misled him; like he wanted to identify himself as a co-founder of creating it but then "Oh you misled me into philanthropic donation with a later plan to turn this into commercial." And having been around it, I know it's factually incorrect. We'll see what happens. It seems that it's like—it's the definition of legal harassment and trying anything possible to kind of say, "No, I didn't make a huge mistake when I basically told OpenAI that it should become a company that I, Elon, should own 80% north of, and if you're not going to do it, I'm leaving." And "Oh look, the organization did well." So I'm hoping the truth and justice will prevail, which is basically it'll be a speedy trial, where all of this stuff will get kicked out and maybe even an OpenAI countersuit for massive defamation and false suits might even work, but we'll see. I certainly wouldn't buy in the SpaceX IPO. I think most of the people who are buying there are kind of in the "Hey, everyone thought Tesla was crazy for a long time and I made a bunch of money on Tesla." And they did. Yes, exactly. And so, what the hell, give it a shot, is I think roughly what the investment thesis is there, which, you know, it's kind of like—that strikes me as like a Bitcoin charter, like "Well, it went up so I should buy because it will go up again," which is again not smart investing behavior. I also a friend of mine kind of said, "Hey, I think what he thinks is going to happen is..." and he's heard from other people. He thinks that SpaceX will go out and then they're going to merge it with Tesla because, you know, Elon's comp package is apparently on market cap, so, you know, gets a whole bunch of money if it goes out and they merge. Can America trust AI? David Sacks makes the case.

POLITICO(211.0K) ·41:40 ·383 views · 2026-05-01
David Sacks (Special Advisor to the President)
David Sacks, special advisor to President Trump on AI, argues for a "let them cook" regulatory philosophy of permissionless innovation to ensure American dominance in a global race against China, emphasizing that AI will ultimately solve the very problems it creates.
TL;DR David Sacks, special advisor to President Trump on AI, argues for a "let them cook" regulatory philosophy of permissionless innovation to ensure American dominance in a global race against China, emphasizing that AI will ultimately solve the very problems it creates. ...

TL;DR

David Sacks, special advisor to President Trump on AI, argues for a "let them cook" regulatory philosophy of permissionless innovation to ensure American dominance in a global race against China, emphasizing that AI will ultimately solve the very problems it creates.

The Trump Administration’s "Permissionless Innovation" Framework

  • Global Competition: Sacks views AI as an "infinite game" where the U.S. cannot afford to slow down; unilateral disarmament would simply cede leadership to China, where half of the world’s AI developers are located.
  • The Three Pillars of AI Policy:
      • Pro-Innovation: Adopting a "let them cook" philosophy that favors private sector leadership with minimally burdensome federal regulation.
      • Pro-Infrastructure: Aligning AI needs with "drill, baby, drill" energy policies to meet the massive power demands of data centers.
      • Pro-Export: Ensuring American chips and models maintain global market share to preserve U.S. "soft power."
  • Preempting State Regulation: The White House framework aims to provide a national alternative to the 1,200+ bills currently moving through state legislatures, which Sacks characterizes as "knee-jerk regulation."

Targeted Regulation and Public Safety

  • Parental Empowerment: Regarding online child safety and AI chatbots, Sacks argues the "North Star" should be giving parents the tools to decide app usage and screen time rather than government bans.
  • Ratepayer Protection: To combat local opposition to data centers (NIMBYism), the administration secured a pledge from major AI companies to ensure new data centers do not increase residential electricity prices.
      • The "Quid Pro Quo": AI companies are encouraged to become "power companies," bringing their own energy sources in exchange for streamlined building permits.
  • Liability Standards: Sacks opposes holding AI developers liable for the actions of end-users, comparing it to holding Microsoft Excel responsible for a money launderer’s spreadsheets. He supports "red teaming" to prevent models from providing instructions for bioweapons or nuclear devices.

National Security and the "AI Equilibrium"

  • The Pentagon Chain of Command: Sacks criticizes AI companies (specifically citing the Anthropic dispute) that attempt to veto the "lawful uses" of their products by the military. He argues that companies selling to the Department of Defense must respect the established chain of command.
  • Cybersecurity Arms Race: Regarding "Mythos," a potent hacking tool held back from public release, Sacks believes the market will reach a new equilibrium.
      • Defensive Parity: He argues that AI's ability to find coding vulnerabilities will be used by "white hats" to patch systems before "black hats" can exploit them.
  • Orwellian vs. Terminator Risks: Sacks is less concerned about "The Terminator" and more concerned with an "Orwellian" future where government and corporate power merge to surveil and censor citizens.

Economic Outlook and the "Pessimism Gap"

  • Debunking Job Loss Narratives: Sacks claims there is "no discernible disruption" to the labor market yet, citing that less than 5% of recent layoffs are attributable to AI.
      • The Construction Boom: He highlights a massive $650 billion investment in CAPEX, which has led to a 25-30% wage increase for blue-collar workers (electricians, plumbers, and drywallers) building AI infrastructure.
  • White-Collar Transformation: While acknowledging shifts in coding and research, Sacks views AI as a "productivity explosion" and a leveler for young workers, rather than a tool for total job evaporation.
  • The Optimism Deficit: Sacks identifies public fear as the biggest threat to U.S. leadership. He notes that while 83% of Chinese citizens are "AI-optimistic," only 39% of Americans share that view.

— David Sacks

— David Sacks

AI is going to solve the problem that AI creates. That's kind of my view on where that market is going. Hello, hello and welcome to The Conversation. I'm Dasha Burns and on this show, I talk to the most compelling and sometimes unexpected power players in Washington and beyond. And this week, I sat down with David Sacks, special advisor to President Trump on all things AI. In the tech world, there's new concerns over the safety of artificial intelligence. As more companies integrate artificial intelligence into their business models, a new analysis is diving deep into just how much the technology could impact the workforce. Lawmakers are getting insights from industry leaders about security issues presented by today's rapidly advancing technology. What happens when you combine artificial intelligence with deadly weapons? As we see the rapid expansion and adoption of artificial intelligence, Sacks has played a key role in shaping policy around the burgeoning technology. He's a venture capitalist and a Silicon Valley veteran who previously held the title of White House AI and crypto czar. So, when it comes to regulating AI, Sacks approaches the issue with a quote "let them cook" philosophy, meaning he thinks the way for the United States to win the global AI race is to move fast with minimally disruptive regulation. But with that approach comes a lot of questions about disruption to the workforce, lawsuits around problems AI has created, increased energy grid demands, all set to the backdrop of public fears around AI. So, we had plenty to discuss. David Sacks joins the conversation. David Sacks, thank you so much for joining the conversation. Good to be here, thank you. I'm so excited to have you on the show because AI is consuming my brain, I think a lot of people's brains, it's a huge priority for the White House and you have been the guy that has been helping build out the framework, set the agenda of how the US should really handle this. I want to start with that White House AI regulatory framework. So it has these legislative recommendations guided by a vision of quote "permissionless innovation" and quote "minimally burdensome regulation". Now, I've heard your spiel on this, but for some of those that might not be as familiar, I do want you to sort of set out why you think that a technology this powerful, this disruptive, should be left mostly in the hands of private companies to control. Why the "let them cook" philosophy, as you call it? Well, I think the first thing to recognize is that we are in a globally competitive environment. We're not the only country that has advanced AI labs. And as the president declared in a major policy speech he gave on AI in July last year, we have to win this AI race. And I'm not sure it has a definite finish line; some people have said it's more of an infinite game. Either way though, we don't want to fall behind our global competitors because that will have a huge impact on our national security and on our economy. So I think that's the first thing to recognize is that if somehow we slow down or stop AI development, it doesn't mean that AI progress is going to stop, it just means it's going to happen in other countries and specifically China. Something like half the world's AI developers are in China or are Chinese. They have the technological expertise, capabilities, and a lot of the talent. So again, it is just globally competitive and it's not like we can just unilaterally disarm or stop AI development. So I think that's sort of the place to start. And then in that important AI speech, the president laid out a few of his pillars on how we win this AI race. And one of them is being pro-innovation. Doesn't mean we don't have to have any regulations, but generally speaking, we should be pro-innovation. In the United States, the innovation comes from the private sector. Ultimately, it's our companies that push the frontier forward. And so when I say "let them cook", I mean that's really what I'm talking about is trying to have a generally encouraging attitude towards innovation. So that would be pillar number one. And then the president also laid out a pro-infrastructure pillar, which really means being pro-energy, which is something that he's talked about for a decade. That's the "drill, baby, drill", yeah. Right, exactly. And so I think he anticipated the need for energy. Energy is the basis not just for data centers but for our entire economy. Fortunately, he's been pushing for this pro-energy policy for a long time and it's never been more relevant or necessary than now. And then I think the third pillar that he talked about was being pro-export. We want the United States to win this race. The way that you measure winning, I think, in a globally competitive market is based on market share. If in five years we look around the world and all the data centers are running on Huawei chips and DeepSeek models, that means that we lost. We don't want to have that future. What we want to see is that the whole world is running on American chips and American models. That would lead to the best economic results for the United States, it would also lead to the United States having more soft power in this area. So that was another important pillar. And I think it's just important that there are smart things we can do with respect to exports. Obviously we don't want to ship our, you know, leading-edge semiconductors to China or something like that. But generally speaking, we want to help encourage our companies to have the greatest market share. So those were sort of the big pillars of the AI framework. And then let me pause there and then I can tell you how we got to the regulatory framework that we just introduced a few weeks ago. Maybe as part of that you can answer my next question, which was, you mentioned that you're not saying that there shouldn't be any regulation, so I'm wondering where you do see the government's role in regulating this tech and what baseline safety requirements should AI systems meet? Well, what's basically happened over the last year is that a lot of the action moved to the state level. And so state legislatures have been taking up the issue. In fact, there's something like 1,200 bills going through state legislatures right now. Our feeling on that is we don't need that many bills. I mean, a lot of this regulation is sort of knee-jerk regulation, although there are some areas where the states have regulated where we think it's a good idea, or could be a good idea. And so what we did is we looked at all of those and we identified a number of principles that we then included in our framework. So one of them is around online child safety; that's a really salient issue. I think mostly it came from the whole social media debate, but it's also relevant for AI as well with, you know, AI chatbots. So we've said this is a legitimate area for states to regulate. Our north star on this issue is parental empowerment. I mean, ultimately we think it's up to parents to decide what apps their kids should use and for how long, you know, screen time limits and things like that. And so, you know, we would let parents decide whether their kids use AI chatbots and what apps they install and how long they get to use them for. I think, you know, child safety is one area. There's another around communities. You know, with respect to data centers, which is another issue that's become very salient for people, we don't want consumer ratepayers to have to pay for higher electricity prices because of AI data centers. We understand that that would not be popular at all and there's a lot of concerns about that. We've supported this idea of a ratepayer protection pledge, which we got all the major AI companies and hyperscalers to sign up for, in which they agree that for any new data centers that they build, it will not increase residential electricity prices. The quid pro quo for that is making it easier for AI companies to build these data centers if they bring their own power. So that's sort of what the president has talked about is we want our AI companies to become power companies. We think that's a much better way of addressing voter concerns than just having a complete ban on data centers the way that Bernie Sanders is pushing. That would just stop the progress altogether. So that's another area we've talked about in our framework. There's also some pillars around creators, making sure they're protected, and there's some pillars around innovation. Again, we tried to go through all the different areas that the states were regulating and put together our list of principles. There's a lot there. There's so many bills and there's also, I mean, you are seeing this, I don't know if it's a battle but like this process getting navigated both by state legislatures at the national level but also in the courts. There's been this growing wave of AI liability lawsuits across the country. You know, Florida is suing OpenAI after a gunman consulted ChatGPT before carrying out a mass shooting. Other courts have alleged that interactions with ChatGPT result in severe mental health issues, delusions, suicide, and that chat has played a role in encouraging people to do horrible things. Should OpenAI or others be held responsible in any of those cases? Well, I guess it sort of depends. First of all, what's the causation and then what's the liability? I don't necessarily want to judge any of those cases too specifically because I haven't gotten into the fact patterns. And I'm not asking you to play lawyer here, but you've seen these trends, sort of the darker side of AI, similar to what we saw with social media, but obviously AI is a whole other beast. So where do you see that part of online safety? Well, I think people are very concerned about kid safety online and that's a concern that we share. And again, this is where we think that the north star should be parental empowerment. Let the parents decide what their kids use. I should say that there's something like over a billion people who are using AI successfully every day now. It's one of the most quickly adopted product releases in history. I guess we're now about four years into this roughly since the launch of ChatGPT. And so I do think that there are these edge cases, there are these horror stories. I mean, I think that does always happen with a technology that's so rapidly adopted by so many people that there are going to be these anecdotes. I don't think that they've been super common, but yeah, they're out there and I do think that the AI companies have already learned from some of these things. There are these cases of teenagers who had histories of mental health challenges who after using an AI chatbot may have, you know, engaged in self-harm. And I think the AI companies have learned from that and now they're doing a lot more where if they detect that that might be the fact pattern that they will do things like obviously not be encouraging towards that behavior, but maybe show numbers for a suicide prevention hotline and things like that. But like basic things, like should AI companies prevent people from being able to use ChatGPT or these chatbots to like commit a crime or learn how to make a bomb or, you know, things like that? I mean, if they know, they probably should stop it. But the problem is just how do you know? I think it's very hard to know exactly all the ways that their product is being used. And I think that is one of the problems with some of these state liability laws that make the developer liable for the actions of the end user. I just don't think that the developer is in a position to know exactly how their product is being used in the same way that Gmail, for example, doesn't necessarily know whether, you know, its email service is being used in the commission of a crime. I think it would be a very hard thing for Google to figure out is just how is email being used. Or let's say that a criminal, you know, let's say a money launderer uses Microsoft Excel; I mean they use a spreadsheet and it could be a cloud-based one to help perpetrate their crime. It's just very hard to know exactly how the end user is using these products. I mean this is why they have terms of use and they don't want their users using them for unlawful purposes. But you don't think that the developers or these AI companies should be held liable in most of these cases that we've talked about? Well, I don't know, you have to tell me exactly what cases we're talking about. But what I would say is that, I mean, should AI models give you step-by-step instructions on how to create a nuclear bomb or a bioweapon? No. I mean, obviously not. We don't want them doing that. But that's something that's been identified as a potential misuse very, very early on. I mean, going back to even before 2023, and that's why all these major AI labs have red teaming and they test for that kind of stuff to make sure. Are they going to be successful at preventing users from being able to use their tools in ways that we don't like? Probably never going to be 100%. I know you probably get this question a lot, so don't roll your eyes at me, but I am curious. You know, Elon Musk, close friend of yours, he's one of the biggest AI entrepreneurs in the industry right now. I mean, I think he is basically in a place where he acknowledges that AI is going to be developed regardless. And so what we want to do is remain in control of it. I mean, I don't think we want to hamstring US development to such a point where again, then China just wins the AI race. So I think he understands that the technology is moving forward and we should develop it in a way that allows us to remain in control. So an area, for example, that I agree with him on is we want the AI to be as truthful as possible. And he's pointed out that if you teach the AI to lie, like let's say, you know, you teach it to be woke or whatever, that's very dangerous because then the AI can lie to us about what it's doing. That's not what we want to inculcate in AI. I very much agree with that. By the way, it's not like I don't think there's any potential dangers or risks of AI. I mean, I do acknowledge that there are potential dystopian futures we don't want. In my view, the most likely dystopian future would be something described by George Orwell in 1984 as opposed to James Cameron in The Terminator. My view of it is that the biggest danger is that the government will ultimately use AI to surveil us, control us, censor us; like that kind of thing. And to me, that should be the thing that we should be most afraid of is a marriage of government and corporate power around AI. By the way, that is the path that I think we were on before President Trump's election. If you go back to the Biden executive order on AI, there were really two major things that it did. One was it said that DEI values, which is to say all the woke stuff, should be promoted in AI models. Are all agencies going to be free to keep using the company's products in perpetuity? And what do you make of that government versus AI narrative? I think that if you're an AI company and you don't want your product to be used in war, then don't sell to the Department of War. I mean, that's kind of in the name, you know? But they did make a decision that they wanted to be used by the Department of War, they do want to be used throughout the government, they made that decision. And then they tried to insert themselves in the chain of command by having a veto over the lawful uses of their product. By the way, just let me back up and just say that I'm not involved in government procurement, right? So I'm a policy advisor; I was not involved in the Anthropic dispute with the Pentagon, I'm still not involved in it. But just if you want my opinion, I just don't think it was a very realistic position for them to take to say that they can dictate to a government agency what lawful uses of their product should be allowed. I just think you have to make a decision about whether you're going to sell to that government agency or or not. I do think they did try to set themselves up in a way of being superior to the chain of command. So you think the concern was valid, but they didn't like execute on that concern properly, or what? Well, yeah, I think if you have a concern about there being loopholes in the law, then let's try and change the law. You don't try and do it through a terms of use negotiation with the Pentagon; that's not going to work. I mean, in other words, they're trying to strong-arm the government into agreeing to their changes to the law in exchange for their product. I mean, that's effectively what they're saying they want to do. But look, if they had talked to me about that issue, and by the way, they never did. Do you think that the government though going after an AI company when you talk about AI dominance and the AI race, like does that hurt American AI dominance when the government is in a fight with one of the major companies fueling the AI race? Well, I don't want to comment on their lawsuit specifically. But I think it happens all the time that, you know, you can have companies work with the government, one department of the government, while another department is suing the government. I mean, I think these things happen all the time. Let's talk about Mythos, another part of Anthropic's work. They recently announced Mythos as this new cybersecurity tool, which it says has unprecedented hacking capabilities and is too dangerous to release publicly. How big of a national security threat is this? I think ultimately if everyone does what they're supposed to do, it won't be a national security threat. But here's basically what's going on, is we have this area of cybersecurity where you've got, you know, offense and defense, you've got the white hats versus the black hats, and basically it's all about preventing hackers from breaching systems, right? They try to break in, they try to steal data, maybe plant back doors, do other nefarious things. And you have this arms race between again, cyber offense and cyber defense. Now, AI is going to be a major part of cybersecurity moving forward. Meaning that you're going to have hackers who are powered up by AI models, but then you've got cyber defenders who are powered up by AI capabilities as well. I think that the cybersecurity market will eventually reach a new equilibrium in which both offense and defense have AI capabilities and therefore the danger won't be ratcheted up to a point where we can't control it, right? So public access to Mythos, you don't think is as big of a national security concern? Well no, no, no, no, no, listen, I think holding it back was the right decision because again, because where I'm going with, because I would not want the hackers to have access to it before the defenders, right? What I'm saying is that I believe that the market will eventually reach a new equilibrium in which there's AI capabilities on the part of both hackers and then also cyber defense. And you know, again, AI is going to solve the problem that AI creates. I mean, that's kind of my view on where that market is going. I was going to say, that sounds like the sort of tagline for your AI theory, that AI will solve the problems AI creates. Well, it may not solve every problem. But I do think in the case of hacking, it will. And look, I think that Mythos is a good example. By the way, I think there were multiple reasons why Anthropic held back that model. I mean, supposedly it's like a 10 trillion parameter model, which just means that it's very, very big, it's very expensive. I'm not sure they had the compute to serve that commercially. So I don't know that a commercial release was really even feasible for them. But in any event, they're giving limited access or a preview to, you know, a couple of dozen companies and the government. And what the people who are in this Project Glasswing will do is they're going to use the capabilities to scan their code bases, look for vulnerabilities, and then patch them. So they're going to harden their code bases. Ultimately, Mythos isn't the only AI model that will do this. I mean, all the major AI companies are working on this now. It's really an extension of coding capabilities. So what happened first is that the AI models got really good at coding, and then as a result of that they got good at finding bugs, and then some of those bugs are vulnerabilities, and some of those vulnerabilities can be strung together into an exploit. So what you want to do is use these new AI coding models to find the bugs and the vulnerabilities and patch them before a hacker does the same thing. And I think the net result of it is that we're going to find a lot of dormant vulnerabilities that were there in the software, maybe for a long time, that no one had found before. As long as everyone does what they're supposed to do, which is the CISOs and the IT departments use these new tools to patch the bugs, then actually it will harden cybersecurity around our core systems because those vulnerabilities won't be there anymore. So to me, the "as long as everyone does what you're supposed to do" is like the red flag clause there because Anthropic decided to go and give the government a heads up and to, you know, to hold this back for multiple reasons potentially. You said it yourself, Mythos is not going to be the last AI model that can do something like this or that can be wielded as a massive cyber weapon against national security or financial data. And you know, future companies or other companies that exist now that make something like this might not necessarily handle it in this same way. Like, don't you think that's a ton of power to be in the hands of private profit-minded companies? Like there's no law that says like "Anthropic, you gotta go talk to the government about this before releasing it." I think that the community is generally aware of that, that these AI coding models are getting better and better and they have this trajectory; they're heading in this direction. So I think cybersecurity is an area where everyone in that community knew that AI was going to have a gigantic impact. I think Mythos, like I said, I think maybe it was too big a model to be commercially viable, but it was very valuable as a proof of concept of what an AI-powered cyber model could do. And so, look, I think it has served a useful purpose in terms of creating popular awareness that companies that are in possession of large code bases and the government potentially are going to need to, over the next say six months, harden their systems, use these capabilities to scan their code, find their vulnerabilities and fix them before a bad guy does the same thing. Doesn't that freak you out a little bit? Well, I think if everyone's asleep at the wheel then it would freak me out, but I think people are pretty alert at this point. You gotta have a lot of faith in these CEOs to not be totally freaked here, I think. Well, or their CISOs. I mean, the nightmare of every Chief Information Security Officer is that their system gets hacked. I mean, that's why they get fired. So I do think that they have a fair amount of urgency and they've been clued into this and I've gotten calls now and outreach from private companies that are trying to get into the Glasswing pilot. They're trying to get access to Mythos, and there's just not enough capacity to give it to everyone who wants it. I do think that Mythos won't be the last model that has cyber capabilities. I mean, all the models that are good at coding will be good at cyber. And so it's just a matter, I think, of, you know, making sure that the people who are playing defense get ahead of this. And I do think it is smart to not just make these capabilities publicly available before again the defense has had a chance to incorporate the lessons. I want to talk about the politics of AI for a minute because, you know, you talked about Orwell, you talked about James Cameron; they're different visions of this, but both of them make people pretty nervous. Poll after poll here in the US shows that a lot of people feel concerned, more concerned than excited about AI. I was looking at a poll that said nearly 75% of Americans think the government isn't doing enough to regulate it and a majority of Americans oppose building AI data centers in their communities. Is the administration out of touch with Americans on this issue? I don't think so. I mean, I think we already know where these things are at. I mean, let's just take the data centers as an example. So we know that there is a strong NIMBY backlash right now to the data centers and one of the big reasons for that is that people are afraid that these data centers are going to make their electricity prices go up. And it's true that if a data center plugs into the grid in a local community without adding power and just is a draw on power, that could make rates go up, absolutely. There's no reason for a community to want that data center if it's going to make their electricity prices go up. The question is how you react to that, right? I mean, I think we all see that unfettered, unrestricted data centers with no conditions is not popular. One reaction to it is just to ban the data centers. I mean, that's what Bernie Sanders wants to do. I think the President's approach is a lot better, which is the obvious one, which is to say, look, if you want to build a data center, you have to bring your own power. And in fact, that not only protects ratepayers against price increases, but it could bring their prices down because when the data centers build their own power, they can sell back to the grid when they're not at peak usage and also they have an incentive to pay for grid upgrades. So, you know, with scale, the more scale you have in general, the more that brings prices down. I think that is just one piece of the concern though. I mean overall Americans don't really trust AI. Like, what can and should companies and governments do to build that trust? Well, look, I agree with you. I mean, one of the things I've said, you know, one of the things the President said is that America's ahead of China by a lot. I say that America's ahead in every category except one, which is optimism. Stanford did a study internationally of different populations' views of AI and they asked people "do you think this will be more beneficial than harmful?" China was something like 83% of the population was AI-optimistic. Oh we're, we're way below that, right? Yeah, I think we're at like 39%. So we're under 40 and they're over 80. And that impacts this race that you're talking about, right? Public perception matters. Totally. No, I think it's probably the biggest threat to our winning the AI race, or at least our leadership in it, which is that we might, out of fear or the public's mistrust of AI, do something that kind of shoots ourselves in the foot. And by the way, I'm not saying that we shouldn't do anything. I mean, again, I think we should have targeted solutions to the problems that are raised, like we just talked about with the data centers, like we talked about with online child safety and self-harm, and like we talked about with cybersecurity. I just think that we should have targeted solutions to specific harms that are raised as opposed to, you know, operating in this environment of sort of vague fear. Because it's not really clear exactly what it is that the people who are really afraid want, other than for everything just to stop. You know, that's like King Canute ordering the tides to stop, right? The tides are not going to stop; China's not going to stop. So I just think we have to be realistic here and I just think that again, as we identify specific harms, we should have specific solutions. There's also been, you know, these cases of violence around AI. You know, a man was charged with attempted murder for an attack against the home of OpenAI CEO Sam Altman. He allegedly carried an anti-AI manifesto and a list of other AI executives to target.
