Well, I mean, anytime there's violence like that, it's totally unacceptable. And I do think it is a foreseeable consequence of some of the overheated rhetoric around AI. I don't think that AI poses all the safety issues it's been accused of and I think it's, you know, beyond wrong to try and take that out on a corporate executive. It's an example of how the rhetoric on this is out of control. I don't blame the media exclusively for this, but I do think that the media is way too focused on the negatives of this technology as opposed to the positives. There's a lot of positives right now for the economy, for economic growth, for productivity, for medical treatments and cures, and it doesn't get the coverage that, say, the coverage around job fears gets. By the way, there's also, let's just talk about the job loss fears for a second. Yeah, we kind of can't not talk about the job part of this, because for everyday Americans, like, that's a huge concern. It is a huge concern, but is there data to support it? And I would argue, no. Now, we can debate what's going to happen in the future, but I don't think the media accurately presents the current state of things. There's a study by Yale, the Yale Budget Lab, which concluded no discernible disruption to the labor market in the first three years after the launch of ChatGPT. So far they have not found a disruption to the labor market. You look at things like the Challenger Grey report, which tallies up the number of layoffs in the economy; less than 5% of the layoffs that were announced last year were attributable to AI. This is a relatively small number. You know, we have over 100 million people working in the American economy, I think something like 150 million, and only 50,000 layoffs last year were attributable to AI. It just was not a big number. And meanwhile, we never talk about the job gains. So we are seeing a construction boom right now that is leading to a boom in blue-collar jobs in the construction industry. You have $650 billion being spent on CAPEX just this year. That's a 2% tailwind to GDP. You're seeing the wages of jobs like electricians, plumbers, the workers who hang drywall or pour concrete or install equipment, build roads; their wages are up 25 to 30%. They can't even hire enough of them. We have a shortage of blue-collar workers. Well, that's been the case for a long time, right? Like my theory, and, you know, you can correct me if I'm wrong here, it is just a theory, but is that like it is going to be white-collar jobs that are more impacted by AI than blue-collar. Like I think all the time about, you know, the junior staff in newsrooms for example, or in Senate offices or wherever, putting together a research packet, like writing a basic breaking news article, doing some of that like intellectual labor but really baseline intellectual labor where pretty soon, maybe even right now, like ChatGPT or Claude or any of these chatbots could do as good or even a better job than like a kid right out of college. But like the way that kid right out of college becomes a senior person at a company is by doing that grunt work first, and then, you know, it's that pipeline that I think is going to get really disrupted by AI. How do you protect that, when, you know, companies are going to look at this as a, you know, why would I spend money to hire a bunch of junior kids when I can have AI do this for me? Well, I think AI is good at automating some tasks, but it's very hard to automate an entire job away. It's hard to automate away a purpose. With respect to these young hires, a lot of them know how to use AI very well. They're AI natives, which you could argue makes them more productive and it's a leveler because they're able to contribute more quickly in the workplace. That is a question we should be asking a lot is just given the challenge of the technology, what do you do about it? My view is that you have to lean in; you want our teenagers for example to be using these tools. It's not something that we should be acting like we want to protect them from. Yes, there are specific harms to protect them from, but in general, I mean I would hate for us to treat AI the way that we sometimes talk about social media, which is it's just harmful and we have to try and get our teenagers to spend as little time on it as possible. I just think it's a very different medium and I think we want our, you know, next generation of workers to be AI natives. I think that's going to make them more productive. Now, with respect to your point about white-collar workers, so I agree with you; that's where the impact is going to be. But let's just say, I think when people start making these sweeping generalizations about massive job loss, I think they're just oversimplifying what's going on. Let me give you an example: so coders. The breakout application of AI models right now beyond just the chatbots has been in coding. This is the thing that's really driving the usage forward right now is that the AI models have gotten extremely good at coding. Anthropic's revenue is exploding because of this. I've even tried vibe coding, David; it's been very exciting for me. Exactly. So you would think, okay, well if AI models are really good at coding, we won't need coders anymore. But if you look at the demand for software engineers, the last study I saw showed that the number of job recs was up 10% year over year. You think that's gonna last though or is that just lagging behind a little bit? Well, you still need someone to prompt the model; you still need someone to supervise. But what if like a Dasha Burns can prompt, and you know, I have zero understanding of coding and I can make an app on my own? I think a lot of small businesses for example would have hired a coder or an engineer to develop something for them and now they can do it on their own, which on the one hand is great for the small business, but on the other hand is not great for the other small business or employee that would have otherwise had a job. Well, I think you're right that the coding capabilities allow companies that previously weren't able to hire software developers to now generate code, which I think will just unleash a productivity explosion for the economy because we can just use software a lot more. But I don't think that's going to put software developers out of work. For one thing, there was a huge latent demand for software developers. I mean, I'm based in Silicon Valley and as long as I can remember, we haven't been able to hire enough software developers for all the companies, all the startups that are operating here. And this has historically been the most attractive place for software developers to go. So if Silicon Valley can't hire enough software developers, that's even more true in the rest of the economy. So look, I think what's happening is that for the first time, large swaths of the economy actually get access to software developers and access to the ability to generate code and to create their own bespoke applications. Now, you asked "will this put coders out of business completely?" I think the answer to that is no. You still need someone to look under the hood. If there's a problem, just because you drive a car doesn't mean you necessarily know how to fix it if something breaks, and you still need someone who knows how to do that. Moreover, if you try to take your bespoke application that you've vibe coded and it's, you know, maybe it's running on your computer and you try to turn that into a production-grade service that you offer on the internet, you're gonna start running into issues. They're not necessarily insurmountable, but you're going to need a coder or you're going to need someone who's very technologically sophisticated to help you solve those problems. So, again, I think this is why we're not seeing the total evaporation of coding jobs; they're shifting their activities from writing code—they're writing less code—but they're doing more, you could say, management of the new AI agents. They're prompting the models more, and they're supervising more. So David, I think it's safe to say given the data around American pessimism versus optimism on AI, that like most average people are not quite where you are with the excitement about what AI can do. Given that, I mean, do you think there could be a downside to how much this administration has leaned into AI when it comes to midterms? Could the fears that people have harm Republicans in 2026 and potentially in 2028 if the White House and this administration are going so hard into this? Well, we have put forward a national AI regulatory framework. You're right that if you poll people and say "do you want to see AI more regulated or less", they will say more. But if you ask them "do you want the federal government to do it or your state", they will actually say the federal government. So we have put forward a national framework; we don't think it should be excessively onerous. We think it should be targeted at specific problems, but I do think we are being responsive to the concerns. I don't think we're ignoring them. But I think at the end of the day, the thing that's going to be most important to voters is going to be the state of the economy. And I do think that because of President Trump's leadership, you are seeing an investment boom. I mean, again, like I mentioned $650 billion of CAPEX in data centers just this year. That is just around the construction part of it; that doesn't include the economic activity that's being generated by the tokens inside the data center. And you're just starting to see that take off, like I mentioned with coding. Anthropic's revenue, which is all based on coding and enterprise, has scaled from something like 10 billion to 30 billion from January to March. It's really unprecedented. And with that, I mean, again, that's just their share of the economic activity. You have to think about well, who's buying those tokens? It's enterprises, it's small businesses, companies that are now building their own software and incorporating that into their business processes. So again, we're just at the outset of unleashing this business productivity explosion. And I understand the concerns about job loss and we're going to be paying attention to those things and really monitoring them, but again so far we do not see that in the data. I mean, you can say that it's going to happen in the future, but it has not happened yet. ‘Write-Downs To Zero’: The $1.8 Trillion Private Credit Warning - Danielle DiMartino Booth

Kitco NEWS(767.0K) ·23:30 ·6.6K views · 2026-05-01
Danielle DiMartino Booth (CEO and Chief Strategist, QI Research)
Despite surface-level market optimism, Danielle DiMartino Booth warns of an imminent "industrial recession" and a looming liquidity crisis in the $1.8 trillion private credit market as corporate margins collapse and actual labor data reveals significant middle-class distress.
TL;DR Despite surface-level market optimism, Danielle DiMartino Booth warns of an imminent "industrial recession" and a looming liquidity crisis in the $1.8 trillion private credit market as corporate margins collapse and actual labor data reveals significant middle-class...

TL;DR

Despite surface-level market optimism, Danielle DiMartino Booth warns of an imminent "industrial recession" and a looming liquidity crisis in the $1.8 trillion private credit market as corporate margins collapse and actual labor data reveals significant middle-class distress.

The Federal Reserve’s "Fractured" Leadership

  • Unprecedented Dissent: The recent 8-to-4 vote on policy represents the largest committee split since 1992, signaling a loss of consensus as inflation remains sticky.
  • Transition to Kevin Warsh: Incoming Chair Kevin Warsh (expected mid-May) inherits a "fractured" Fed.
      • Powell's Role: Outgoing Chair Jay Powell has signaled he will step back into a governor role and remain deferential, avoiding becoming a "shadow chair."
      • Alternative Data: Warsh is expected to utilize alternative data sources—such as small business bankruptcies—rather than traditional metrics to justify interest rate cuts.
  • Policy Lag: Booth argues the Fed is already "too late" to the easing process, noting that payroll revisions show net job destruction in the second and third quarters of 2025.

The $1.8 Trillion Private Credit & CRE Warning

  • Write-Downs to Zero: Booth highlights that firms like Ares Capital are already writing down big investments from par to zero, signaling the end of a seven-year period of low due diligence.
  • Liquidity Run Risks: The Financial Stability Board is investigating the 1.8 trillion-dollar private credit market as retail investors follow institutions into these illiquid assets.
      • Redemption Requests: High redemption requests from "skittish" retail investors could force a broader market sell-off if they cannot exit illiquid private credit positions.
  • Commercial Real Estate (CRE) Distress: With 30-year Treasury yields tapping 5%, the relief regional banks expected from falling rates has vanished.
      • Fire Sales: Office properties are seeing 90% discounts and distressed sales are at a decade high, further tightening lending standards.

The K-Shaped Economy and Labor Market Decay

  • The "Severance Cliff": While jobless claims appear low (189,000), Booth notes that only one in four unemployed Americans are actually collecting benefits, with a 40% exhaustion rate.
      • Underemployment: The job market has shifted toward low-quality, temporary, and contractor roles designed to execute AI implementations that eventually replace permanent staff.
  • Consumer Exhaustion: Subprime and near-prime borrowers are now spending 16% of their monthly income on debt payments.
      • Discretionary Slump: Personal savings have hit their lowest point since 2022, leading to record-low numbers of Americans planning summer road trips.
  • Housing Market Freeze: Mortgage rates returning to 6.3% have frozen the market, but re-listings are rising as layoffs (at firms like Meta and Estée Lauder) force families to monetize their primary assets.

Industrial Recession and Global Capital Shifts

  • Manufacturing Margin Squeeze: US manufacturers are "panic buying" inventory to get ahead of higher input costs and energy crises, while simultaneously slashing labor costs to protect margins.
  • The "Industrial Recession": Booth predicts a "cliff dive" in manufacturing this summer as companies struggle with weaker demand and higher carrying costs.
  • Institutional Pivot to Gold: Crypto entities (like Tether) and central banks are stockpiling physical gold, signaling a breakdown in institutional trust in the US dollar amid fears of "socialist" fiscal policies and persistent double-digit inflation.

Notable Quotes

"We've gone from par to zero. So the cracks continue to emerge, the distress continues to become more apparent... there’s big trouble when you’ve had effectively a seven-year period of very low due diligence." — Danielle DiMartino Booth
"The exhaustion rate is 40%. So we're not actually looking at data through a true prism... only one in four Americans who are unemployed are actually collecting benefits." — Danielle DiMartino Booth
"If the Fed is forced to lower interest rates too late because of what Jamie Dimon is referencing—a potential liquidity event—there's just going to be less money for [retirees] to spend." — Danielle DiMartino Booth
"We are seeing 90% discounts on office properties. Office distressed sales are at a decade high." — Danielle DiMartino Booth
Welcome back. I'm Jeremy Szafron and we're looking at some incredibly conflicting data in the markets today. Equities are extending their longest weekly rally since 2024 and jobless claims have dropped to levels we haven't seen since 1969. Yet the structural numbers point to a very different reality. At the corporate level, Jamie Dimon is out with a fresh warning that a credit-led recession could be much worse than anticipated. It tells us that A, Kashkari is a well-known, notorious dove, and the signal that it sends is good luck forming a consensus to future chair Kevin Warsh. I think that that was the message that they were trying to send. This is not going to be your grandfather's Fed. You're going to have trouble really corralling the ranks and dissent may become part of your job on day one. I think that that really is the signal that it was sending. Jensen Huang & Anirudh Devgan on Design for AI and AI for Design at CadenceLIVE 2026 – Fireside Chat

Cadence Design Systems(41.6K) ·34:22 ·74 views · 2026-05-01
Jensen Huang (Founder and CEO, NVIDIA) Anirudh Devgan (President and CEO, Cadence)
The industry is shifting from generative AI that "knows" to agentic AI that "does," effectively expanding the chip design workforce by 1,000x as virtual agents use specialized EDA tools to solve complex optimization and physical design problems.
TL;DR The industry is shifting from generative AI that "knows" to agentic AI that "does," effectively expanding the chip design workforce by 1,000x as virtual agents use specialized EDA tools to solve complex optimization and physical design problems. The "Knowing" vs. "Doing"...

TL;DR

The industry is shifting from generative AI that "knows" to agentic AI that "does," effectively expanding the chip design workforce by 1,000x as virtual agents use specialized EDA tools to solve complex optimization and physical design problems.

The Shift from Generative to Agentic AI

  • The "Knowing" vs. "Doing" Inflection Point: Jensen Huang emphasized that while generative AI (perception and knowledge) was the first step, the current "agentic" revolution allows AI to reason, execute plans, and use external tools.
  • The "Microwave" Analogy for Tool Use: Huang argued that agents will not reinvent fundamental engineering algorithms but will instead use them as tools.
      • Just as a household robot would use a microwave rather than emitting radiation from its fingers, AI agents will use Cadence’s established EDA tools to verify and refine designs.
  • Scaling Beyond Human Constraints: Historically, the semiconductor industry has been limited by the number of human designers ("butts in seats"). In the agentic era, growth is limited only by compute and token budgets.

AI-Driven Specialized Chip Design Agents

  • Domain-Specific Agents: NVIDIA and Cadence are collaborating on several specialized agents to handle the most labor-intensive parts of chip design:
      • ChipStack: Focuses on RTL (Register Transfer Level) and verification, specializing in complex protocols like PCIe Express and CXL.
      • VeraStack: An agent for analog design, which has traditionally been difficult to automate due to the extreme limits of physics and signal integrity.
  • Physical Design Optimization: Physical design is described as a "many-variable, impossible-to-find-a-perfect-answer" search algorithm.
      • Agents allow for massive parallelization, running thousands of permutations 24/7 to find the best PPA (Power, Performance, Area) solutions.
  • Virtual Engineers: Anirudh Devgan noted that virtual agents are already integrated into NVIDIA’s workflows, participating in Slack channels alongside human engineers to solve specific design bugs.

The Rise of Physical AI and Robotics

  • The "ChatGPT Moment" for Robotics: The industry has arrived at the Vision-Language-Action (VLA) model era. This allows for "pixels-in, action-out" capabilities, enabling robots to understand physicality and reason through complex scenarios.
  • Synthetic Data and World Models: Because physical data is harder to gather than text, NVIDIA and Cadence are using "AI Physics" to generate synthetic data.
      • High-Fidelity Simulation: Cadence’s acquisition of MSC (Adams) provides the high-fidelity physics solvers needed to train NVIDIA’s Cosmos and Isaac platforms.
      • Reasoning in Motion: Huang highlighted "Alpha Mo," a reasoning car that can "think out loud" to navigate complex environments (e.g., deciding to move around a truck parked in its lane).

Post-Agentic Leadership and Business Strategy

  • The Token Budget Paradigm: Huang predicts that future engineering productivity will be measured by token usage.
      • If a team isn't spending hundreds of millions—or even billions—of tokens on agents, they are likely failing to maximize their scope of responsibility.
  • The "Full-Stack" Company: Devgan and Huang agreed that engineering is no longer siloed. Designing a modern chip requires a full-stack understanding of the software, the systems, and even the "AI factories" (data centers) where they reside.
  • Expanded TAM for EDA: By moving from chips into physical systems (robotics, automotive, CFD), Cadence is expanding its Total Addressable Market (TAM) to include every industry that designs physical products.
"People who are 'know-it-alls' are interesting but not valuable. People who can do things are valuable. AI went from knowing everything... to now being able to use tools." — Jensen Huang
"The number of ASIC designers is likely to increase by a thousand times in the near future... In the future, we’re only limited by compute." — Jensen Huang
"One thing remarkable about chip design is how much effort we put in verification... it takes one person to design it, and 100 engineers to fix it. [With agents], my Slack channel will be 99% Cadence." — Anirudh Devgan
"If a company isn't spending billions of dollars of tokens a year, obviously we’re not going to be producing the things that we’re doing. These are new sensibilities that are coming in." — Jensen Huang

And so we plan physical design from practically the beginning of the design process because that's how long it takes. You're in the physical design process for two years. And the reason for that is because of course the amount of technology that... the amount of components that fit into the physical design that you have to place is large. But you have a fundamental bin-packing problem and you're trying to optimize for size, you're optimizing for manufacturability, and you're optimizing for timing. Meanwhile, everything's being designed at the same time. It's an incredibly challenging process. And so now we have agentic systems that help us basically work 24/7. You parallelize the work across thousands of agents. And I'm hoping—and today we're scaling it up to tens and hundreds—but we'd like to scale it up to thousands and hundreds of thousands of agents. They'll all be working around the clock trying all kinds of different permutations so that you could solve this many-variable, impossible-to-find-a-perfect-answer optimization problem. And so this is an ideal place for agents. Analog design is very similar to that. The design space is extremely large and it's hard to know when you're pushing the limits of analog design. We're basically at the limits of physics. And there's a lot of different permutations, a lot of different ways you could design the analog circuitry. Having agents explore the space for us, simultaneously designing for signal integrity, for power, for size, and for manufacturability, exploring all of that simultaneously across all the agents. These are all exploration problems. And so I think the... there's a misunderstanding about fundamental design that you have a problem statement, you come up with a solution like you're solving a puzzle, and that's the answer. Well, it turns out you know there's a thousand—exactly—plausible answers, a million plausible answers. The question is which one are you going to go and optimize towards? Well, having more agents helping you explore the design space, the better. And that has been the history of EDA and chip design. Because like you've said before, there is no one right answer. There are thousands of right answers. You have to find the one with the best PPA. So anything that helps find the best right answer is invaluable in the design process. That's right. It's a search algorithm, and one of the most complex search algorithms in the world. We're going to need a lot of agents to help us do that. Yeah, super exciting. Trump on dissatisfaction with Iran, potential Spirit bailout, King Charles: Full press conference

USA TODAY(7.5M) ·22:37 ·293 views · 2026-05-01
Donald Trump (President)
President Trump expressed dissatisfaction with current negotiation terms with a "disjointed" Iranian leadership, while emphasizing a "USA First" economic agenda characterized by aggressive EU auto tariffs, record domestic job growth, and potential federal intervention to save 14,000 jobs at Spirit.
TL;DR President Trump expressed dissatisfaction with current negotiation terms with a "disjointed" Iranian leadership, while emphasizing a "USA First" economic agenda characterized by aggressive EU auto tariffs, record domestic job growth, and potential federal intervention to...

TL;DR

President Trump expressed dissatisfaction with current negotiation terms with a "disjointed" Iranian leadership, while emphasizing a "USA First" economic agenda characterized by aggressive EU auto tariffs, record domestic job growth, and potential federal intervention to save 14,000 jobs at Spirit.

Iran and Middle East Security

  • Negotiation Status: Iran reportedly seeks a deal due to a decimated military, but the President remains "not satisfied" with current terms.
      • Disjointed Leadership: The President characterized Iranian leadership as divided into three or four argumentative groups, making cohesive negotiation difficult.
      • Military Capability: Trump asserted that Iran’s navy, air force, and radar capabilities have been "wiped out" and its military "defeated."
  • Nuclear Non-Proliferation: A primary red line remains the prevention of Iran acquiring a nuclear weapon, which the President stated would cause "problems the likes of which the world has never seen."
  • Strategic Blockade: The Strait is described as "100 percent shut down" via a "flawless" blockade, which has trapped record amounts of oil in tankers.
  • Regional Support: Trump expressed strong support for the new head of Iraq and maintained "tremendous respect" for the leadership in Pakistan.

Trade and Domestic Manufacturing

  • EU Auto Tariffs: Tariffs on cars and trucks from the European Union were raised to 25% because the EU was "not adhering" to existing trade agreements.
      • Impact: The administration claims these tariffs force foreign manufacturers to move production to the U.S. more quickly.
  • Manufacturing Boom: Over $100 billion in new car plants are currently under construction in the U.S. by companies from Japan, South Korea, Canada, and Mexico.
  • China Relations: The President previewed an upcoming "amazing" trip to China to meet with Chairman Xi, noting that negotiations are ongoing.

Economic Policy and Corporate Assistance

  • Corporate Bailouts: The administration is evaluating a deal to help Spirit (Spirit AeroSystems) to save 14,000 jobs, though Trump emphasized that any deal must be "America First" and provide a return to the U.S.
      • Intel Precedent: Trump cited a previous deal with Intel that allegedly resulted in a $40 billion profit for the United States.
  • Tax Proposals: The President highlighted several key tax initiatives intended to stimulate the economy:
      • No tax on tips or overtime pay.
      • No tax on Social Security for seniors.
      • Tax deductions for interest on loans for American-made cars.
  • Energy Prices: While acknowledging high gasoline prices, the President predicted they would "tumble" significantly once current conflicts end and the oil currently trapped in tankers is released.

Military Authority and Defense

  • War Powers Act: The President dismissed the War Powers Act as "unconstitutional" and "never adhered to" by previous presidents, arguing against its use to restrict his authority.
  • Military Inventory: Trump criticized the Biden administration for giving away military inventory to Ukraine without compensation. He claimed the U.S. military is currently "locked and loaded" with double the inventory held at the start of the current period.
  • UK Relations: Trump spoke warmly of a "high-quality" meeting with King Charles and the Queen, praising the King’s previous work and their current relationship.

Earnings Keep the Market Strong Despite Signs of Consumer Weakness | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(172.0K) ·28:11 ·38.7K views · 2026-05-01
Steve Eisman (Portfolio Manager, Neuberger Berman)
The U.S.
TL;DR The U.S. economy remains bifurcated ("K-shaped"), where massive AI-driven infrastructure spending by Big Tech and industrials continues to propel the market upward despite clear evidence of a recession among low-to-middle-income consumers. The primary driver of current GDP...

TL;DR

The U.S. economy remains bifurcated ("K-shaped"), where massive AI-driven infrastructure spending by Big Tech and industrials continues to propel the market upward despite clear evidence of a recession among low-to-middle-income consumers.

The AI Arms Race and Industrial Beneficiaries

The primary driver of current GDP and market strength is the unabated surge in Capital Expenditure (CapEx) related to artificial intelligence.

  • Big Tech Earnings: Results from Google, Microsoft, Amazon, and Meta confirm no slowdown in AI spending.
      • Google: Significant acceleration in cloud revenue ($20B) drove a 10% stock surge.
      • Microsoft: Azure grew 39%, though the stock softened as investors sought even higher beats.
      • Amazon: AWS revenue rose 28%, and the company reported $225B in revenue commitments for its "Trainium" AI chip.
      • Meta: Despite beating metrics, shares fell after the company raised its 2026 CapEx outlook to $135B, signaling a high-cost battle for AI dominance.
  • Industrial Infrastructure: The "data center boom" is fueling non-tech sectors.
      • Quanta Services: Benefiting from utility construction and maintenance required for AI power needs.
      • Caterpillar: Sales grew 38% in the construction unit, driven largely by data center equipment demand.

The K-Shaped Economy: Consumer Divergence

Earnings reports reveal a sharp divide between the financial health of different consumer segments.

  • Signs of Weakness:
      • Domino’s Pizza: Weak same-store sales (0.9% vs. 2.3% expected) suggest the low-to-middle-end consumer is currently in a "deep recession."
      • Starbucks: Despite a difficult backdrop, reported its second straight quarter of traffic growth, suggesting business changes are finally taking hold.
  • Signs of Strength:
      • Visa: Net revenue rose 17% with payment volumes up 9%, indicating that overall consumer spending remains robust at the higher end of the "K."
      • Eli Lilly: Reported a "blowout" quarter (EPS of $8.55 vs. $6.66 expected), confirming its dominance in the weight-loss drug market.

High-Conviction Plays and Market Risks

Steve Eisman detailed his specific thesis on several controversial or high-profile names.

  • Charter Communications (Conviction Long): Despite a 25% stock drop following a loss of 120,000 broadband subscribers, Eisman is holding and adding to his position.
      • Thesis: CapEx is scheduled to drop from $11B to ~$7.5B by 2028, which should theoretically drive massive free cash flow for stock buybacks.
  • FICO (Conviction Short): Eisman remains short, citing a massive pricing disparity between FICO and its competitor, VantageScore.
      • The Math: For every 100 applications, FICO charges ~$2,049 compared to VantageScore’s ~$99. Eisman believes lenders will inevitably shift toward the lower-cost provider once implementation begins in 2027.
  • Private Credit Concerns: Eisman questioned the "marks" on software loans within private credit portfolios (specifically Ares and Blue Owl).
      • Refinancing Risk: With public software valuations down significantly (ServiceNow used as a proxy), many private equity-backed software firms may find it impossible to refinance loans when their equity value has halved.

Geopolitics and Macro Trends

Global alliances and market concentrations are shifting, creating new layers of uncertainty.

  • The UAE’s Exit from OPEC: A "canary moment" signaling a shift in worldwide alliances. The UAE appears ready to "go it alone" to increase production and grab market share amid the Iran conflict.
  • Energy Prices: Brent crude has climbed above $115 as the U.S. maintains a blockage of the Strait of Hormuz to pressure Iran economically.
  • Market Concentration: Semiconductors now constitute 16% of the S&P 500. This makes the entire index hypersensitive to any negative news regarding AI or data center demand.

***

Beyond Bigger Models: Recursion As The Next Scaling Law In AI

Y Combinator(2.2M) ·37:53 ·3.7K views · 2026-05-01
Ankit Gupta (Y Combinator) Francois Chaubard (Y Combinator)
> "It is actually impossible for the [standard LLM] model to map from an unsorted list to a sorted list on a one-shot basis...
TL;DR: Recursive AI models like Hierarchical Reasoning Models (HRM) and Tiny Recursive Models (TRM) achieve state-of-the-art reasoning performance—outperforming models 1,000x their size on tasks like the ARC Prize—by utilizing weight reuse and latent memory states at inference...

TL;DR: Recursive AI models like Hierarchical Reasoning Models (HRM) and Tiny Recursive Models (TRM) achieve state-of-the-art reasoning performance—outperforming models 1,000x their size on tasks like the ARC Prize—by utilizing weight reuse and latent memory states at inference time to bypass the "reasoning ceiling" of standard feed-forward LLMs.

The Structural Limits of Standard LLMs

  • Feed-Forward Constraints: While modern LLMs excel at next-token prediction, they are essentially one-shot feed-forward processes at training time. This limits their ability to solve "incompressible" problems that require iterative computation.
  • The Sorting Analogy: Francois Chaubard explains that a transformer with 30 layers cannot solve a comparison sort for a 31-element list because the problem has a theoretical lower bound of $N \log N$ steps. Standard models literally "run out of layers" to complete the necessary logic.
  • Discrete vs. Latent Reasoning:
      • Chain of Thought (CoT): This is a "hack" that forces recursion in the visible token space, but it is limited by human-labeled data and training traces.
      • Recursive Latent Space: True recursive models reason within a continuous, high-dimensional latent space (memory), making them more expressive and efficient than models that must "snap back" to discrete tokens.
  • The Einstein Test: Standard models are bounded by the limits of human knowledge. They can use tools (like a Python sort function) because they have seen them before, but they struggle to discover new algorithms (e.g., discovering Merge Sort if they only knew Bubble Sort).

Hierarchical Reasoning Models (HRM)

  • Architecture: Inspired by the human brain's varying processing frequencies, HRM uses three nested levels of recursion:
      • Low-level (L-net): Operates at high frequency for local features.
      • High-level (H-net): Operates at a lower frequency for broader context.
      • Outer Refinement Loop: Scales the reasoning depth at inference time.
  • The "Stop Grad" Innovation: To avoid the "vanishing gradient" problem common in old Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs), HRM uses a fixed-point iteration method.
      • Memory Persistence: The model performs 16 iterations but only backpropagates through a small portion, essentially treating different memory states as a "mini-batch" rather than resetting activations to zero.
  • Performance: With only 27 million parameters and no pre-training, HRM achieved ~70% on the ARC Prize, while massive models like o3 initially scored zero.

Tiny Recursive Models (TRM): Complexity through Simplification

  • Model Efficiency: TRM refines the HRM approach by collapsing the dual networks into a single shared-weight transformer layer. Despite having only 7 million parameters, it outperforms HRM.
  • Architectural Shifts:
      • Weight Sharing: TRM uses the same network for both high and low-level features, proving that variable scoping (distinct memory states) is more important than having distinct physical layers.
      • Deep Supervision: It utilizes "truncated backprop through time," backpropagating through one full recursive loop rather than a single step, which significantly boosts performance.
  • Task Specificity: While small, these models are currently task-specific. A TRM trained for Sudoku cannot inherently solve ARC puzzles, marking a departure from the general-purpose nature of LLMs.

The Future of Scaling: Depth over Width

  • Compute Depth: The "next scaling law" involves increasing compute depth (recursion steps) rather than just parameter width.
  • Merging Paradigms: The ultimate frontier in AI research is combining the representation power of giant foundation models (like Gemini or GPT-4) with the recursive reasoning engines of TRMs.
  • Latent Reasoning: Future architectures will likely move reasoning into the latent "embedding" space rather than relying on the inefficient, token-by-token Chain of Thought method.

Notable Quotes

"It is actually impossible for the [standard LLM] model to map from an unsorted list to a sorted list on a one-shot basis... if I have a list that's 31 elements long and my transformer is 30 [layers], I run out of steps to do comparisons." — Francois Chaubard
"This is why this paper is so exciting: [TRM] is a 7-million parameter model that can solve what a trillion-parameter model can't solve, even when the latter is trained on the entire internet." — Francois Chaubard
"In the case of RNNs in general, they remain in this continuous latent space which is much, much higher dimensional... It’s much more expressive to be in continuous space, but we couldn't train it that way because you're inhibited by backprop through time." — Ankit Gupta
"We found mapping from pixels to some really cool latent space where things are nicely semantically separated... But now in that space, use these tiny reasoning models—use some type of recursion inside that—and I think that’s going to really work." — Francois Chaubard
Welcome back to another episode of Decoded. Today, I'm back with YC Visiting Partner Francois Chaubard to talk about one of the most interesting recent trends in AI research: recursion. Specifically, we're going to talk about how we can improve a model's reasoning performance by using recursion at inference time, rather than by just making the model bigger and bigger. There were two papers that made the power of this approach really clear in 2025. One on Hierarchical Reasoning Models, or HRM, and another on Tiny Recursive Models, or TRM. Francois, thanks for joining us. Can you tell us a little bit about these two models and what was so interesting about them? Sure. I guess, to set up a little bit of a foundation, you already did an amazing lecture on RNNs and LLMs in one of the previous videos, so I won't overdo it. Just to give the cliff notes, an RNN is just a model that you recursively call again and again and again on itself. We were very much in the belief that this was required to get to AGI. Peak RNN use was probably until 2016 with Alex Graves' NeurIPS keynote, which is just fantastic, and all his adaptive compute time work. So this is about 10 years ago that people were working on these models. This was in the era of LSTMs and LSTMs with attention. And, depending on which professors you talk to, this was before attention was invented. Yes, totally. And I think what really was the limiting step on RNNs in general was this thing called backprop through time, where you have to roll out the model, and then to update the weights, you need to approximate the gradient, and you step back, back, back, and you keep rolling out. As the model gets bigger and bigger, and as you roll out for more and more steps, then you have all these accumulation of errors. The gradient gets noisier and noisier, and then it just kind of stops working. Yeah, so you have these vanishing or exploding gradient problems. It's because if you have an input with 20 steps, you're multiplying these matrices 20 times and that causes training problems. And we're talking about doing context lengths of like a million or like a billion. So it's not even just 20; it's like a billion. Right. And even worse, you have to retain the activations at every single step. So if this were happening in your brain, you would need like a million copies of your brain at every single activation so that I can backprop through it. There's tricks around this that you can do, like gradient checkpointing and things like that to reduce that issue, but then you're just trading off memory for wall clock time and compute. Right, so now if you contrast that with LLMs, the ones that people are widely using, while at face value they appear to be similar, at training time they're doing basically this one-shot feed-forward process for every input, right? The LLM, the transformer block, can take all the inputs in parallel. It's not actually iteratively going over them one at a time at train time, so you don't have this problem of needing to store tons of activations or this giant vanishing gradients problem. Yeah, exactly. Like, it's actually all happening in time in one shot magically. That was the lower triangle trick that kind of happens with this causal mask. And so you actually do all time steps in one shot, and you forward pass a feed-forward model on all time steps in one shot and you back-wards in one shot. It's amazing for train time in terms of wall clock. It requires a lot of FLOPs and it still requires a lot of the memory. You still need it there, but you don't have the vanishing gradient issue. And what you actually paid for that you have to give up is this latent reasoning thing and this compression in the time direction. There is no compression in LLMs. Every single decode that I do, I still have to retain the entire Shakespeare novel just to decode a little bit. In RNNs you don't have to do that; it's all compressed in this hidden state that you kind of roll out. Okay, so let's talk about that in a little bit more detail. You refer to this inherent reasoning ability. Many people think about LLMs as doing reasoning, and we're going to talk about that a little bit later, but help me understand where you see the biggest limitations in LLM reasoning ability in terms of what the model does in an actual forward pass. Yeah, so I guess we go back to GPT-2. GPT-2 was this landmark architecture and paper that basically was just "get next token, next token, next token," and it kind of worked. We just watched val loss go down, perplexity go down, and the model just becomes more performant. It looks better and starts to make some Shakespeare that actually sounds somewhat plausible. Right. And then we have to get these things to reason and to actually solve some really hard problems. And I've done extensive experiments on this, but if you take, for example, sort. You have infinite amounts of unsorted lists and you give it sorted lists. You keep feeding it to the model, so it should work, right? It's actually impossible for the model to map from an unsorted list to a sorted list in a one-shot basis. It's literally that we know a theoretical lower bound that for comparison sort you can't do better than N log N steps. And if I have a list that's 31 characters or elements long, and my transformer is 30, I run out of steps to do comparisons. It's not possible for me to do all the steps that need to be done. In HRM and TRM they use Sudoku as an incompressible problem, and so are mazes. Rolling sum is another incompressible problem. When you mentioned the sorting algorithm, when I think back to my algorithms class from college, the one way you could get faster than N log N in a sorting algorithm is if you had some access to an external memory cache. If you had some tape you could write to, then you can actually do faster than N log N by basically selectively putting things into this memory. I suspect that's a key limitation of these LLMs, in that because there's no external memory tape inbuilt into the model, you lose certain performance possibilities in terms of how fast you can go. That's right. And so I guess radix sort would be the most common one. Depending on the number of buckets that you have, you can kind of get from N log N to order N. You can't get less than N; you have to touch all the elements. And if you run out of transformer layers in your neural network, then you ran out of chances to do that. Yeah, so this is just like going back to Alan Turing and a Turing machine, right? What's the analogy there exactly that we should think about in terms of LLMs not quite satisfying how you think about a Turing machine? Well, if we just talk about GPT-2, the original, no bells and whistles version, it's just a feed-forward model. And so it's just forward passing one step and taking an input, creating a bunch of outputs. In the Sudoku case, if I have 50 different potential steps and it's provable that I can only do one given this information, but I only have this many layers, then that's all I can do. And the cheat is the chain of thought. It's completely true that at test time, they are Turing complete and you can simulate all Turing computable functions. But how do you get it to learn it? You need to train it. And that's where, unless you're training it on human-labeled traces, for which there's a lot of problems like the Millennium Prize problem, we don't have the trace for it. Right, we'd love to have the trace for it, but it just doesn't exist. Totally. Makes sense. Okay, so with that context in mind, let's talk about these two papers, because I think that sets up a lot of the contrast we're going to draw between these papers and the models that people are maybe more used to. Let's talk about HRMs first. Walk me through a little bit about how this model works and some of the intuition behind it. Sure. So, this is directly in the lineage of RNNs. There's not that much novel from the RNN standpoint, at least in my opinion. They do have this idea, inspired by the brain, where you have different parts of the brain that operate on different frequencies. There's some that operate at a really high frequency, which is on the low level of the hierarchy, and some that operate at a really low frequency, which is the higher level of the hierarchy. The interplay between those things is really interesting. So this is like literally in the human brain; there's some bio-inspiration here, where you have different waves running at different frequencies at different parts of the brain or something like that. Okay, cool. Yeah. And I guess that interpretation is one way they are talking about classifying these hierarchies of frequencies. The most interesting part, at least for me, is the way that they train the neural network. You take in some X, some input, whether it's an incomplete Sudoku puzzle, a maze, or an ARC Prize challenge. You do TL steps with the L, the low-level module, then you go to H and you do that TH times, and then you have N sup outer refinement steps. Yeah, so you basically are running through the input with a given matrix, with a given transformation repeatedly on it. And you're doing that through two levels of refinement and then basically running that process several times. Yes, so then there's exactly three levels of recursion occurring here. There's the low level, there's the high level, and then there's the outer refinement steps. And we're calling it recursion because it's the same weights that are being applied repeatedly. We're not changing the weights in between these steps. Exactly right. You get to recurse on the L-net TL times, you recurse on the TH and the TL, this looped recursion TH times, and then you do this whole outer refinement step N sup times. Cool. And so what's the basic intuition for why that works? Why does that produce an effective paper result, and what even were the results that this paper showed? Yeah, so I mean this got state-of-the-art on ARC Prize 1 and 2. This was only a 27 million parameter model that was only trained on ARC Prize. So it's like a thousand inputs, like puzzles basically. Yeah, it's literally a thousand tasks, which is extremely small. There is no pre-training at all. This starts from literally tabula rasa weights, and it can outperform models like o3, if you remember back way back when. o3 gets zero, literally zero, and this got like something like 70% on ARC Prize 1 at least, which was just a huge breakthrough. And so you can think of this as variable scoping. If I have three nested functions, I guess the lowest level function has scoped variables, which they'll call Z L, which is the carry that inits at zero. A latent variable, basically. And like in traditional RNN literature they would call this the hidden state, the low-level hidden state. I get to recurse, recurse, recurse, and then I pass back that Z L back to the outer scoped function, the high-level one. I let that one do one iteration, it goes back and calls the low-level again, and it does this whole thing in a third outer loop, which is called the outer refinement step. Okay, but when you describe it like that, it seems like it would have the same backprop through time problem that you would have with RNNs. And I think they came up with a clever trick to basically get around that. So what was that trick that they figured out? This is really the crux of the paper that differentiates it. Instead of doing what Alex Graves did in all of his papers, from neural Turing machines to adaptive compute time to differentiable neural computers, where he always backpropped through all of the recursion steps, they do something different. He was limited by backprop through time, so he could only make the model so big and had issues with vanishing gradients. What they do is they kind of have this DEQ method of doing fixed-point iteration. So that's like deep equilibrium models, yeah cool. Yeah, deep equilibrium learning, where you take a batch and do something completely counterintuitive. As a computer vision person you'd never do this, but it actually does make sense. If I take a batch of like ImageNet and I forward pass through the model and I get some loss and I backprop and I update the weights, I would go get a different batch for the next one. But what they do instead is they actually do that 16 times. As you do that, you actually can see the change in your residuals get less and less. Why it actually makes sense is because in the RNN case, the Z L and the Z H, which are the carry, start out as zeros. Then we go through this whole loopy recursion through the two lower loops, and then I backprop just through the two modules, just once. I don't recurse all the way back; I do a stop grad. I stop right there, and then there's a huge residual, and then I don't reset Z L and Z H. I do it again at a different point in the carry or hidden variable space. So one can actually look at it as a different batch every time, even though it's the same exact X. Yeah, like the way I kind of think about it is like the 16 iterations or whatever that you're recursing over. It's like constructing a mini-batch, not from different inputs, but from different memory states basically. Right, it's like across this hidden or carry memory access. And that math holds and it works. It follows DEQ directly, in the event that the delta in Z L and Z H go to zero. It actually doesn't do that. And so we'll get to TRM, but Alexia basically shows that this is just not the case and you can't actually apply this math. That's not sufficient support for why it's working. We actually don't know why it's really working, and she figures out that you actually can backprop through all the way to the deep recursion, which actually improves performance much, much more. Interesting. Okay, so before we get into TRM, on this paper, I think there's a bunch of different ways people have looked at this in terms of why this may or may not be working. One is the sort of bio-plausibility argument. Now as you know, I'm usually not super keen on these. I think machine learning has a long history of people starting with bio-plausible arguments and then realizing that there's some variant that seems highly bio-implausible that actually works better. I think you have examples along these lines. Yeah, I mean, the classic first deep learning paper that started this whole craziness is AlexNet. In AlexNet there's actually this funny little thing called local receptive activation or depression, where once this activation fires, then I have this refractory region. It actually didn't work at all. It didn't work and you didn't need that. Then VGG came out and said get rid of all that, just go deeper with 3x3 convs, and it actually just outperforms dramatically. So this is always the case. Maybe you need to do it to get accepted into NeurIPS, add some biological plausibility. Sure, totally. You're definitely the expert here, but what do you consider to be bio-plausible and what's not? Well, I think that a lot of machine learning literature has overlapped a lot with people working in neuroscience, and I think it is very natural for us to ask questions about how our brain works. Because our brain is like an incredible instrument that does a ton of computing obviously, and does it in a very shockingly efficient manner. So a lot of machine learning research has for a long time sought analogs from how we think our brain works and tried to encode that in various systems. From the very basic concept of what a neural network is, it's called a neural network because we think it's some basic model for what a neuron is. Do you think that's a misnomer? The thing about them is that often we use bio-plausibility to inspire us to come up with ideas, but we end up veering away from the bio-plausible to something adjacent to them that is likely bio-implausible but seems to work better. And I think that runs better on a GPU. Exactly, it runs better on a GPU; it's more efficient in some capacity that is relevant to how we actually encode it. No, because I needed to know what sort was. What happens if we didn't know what merge sort was? I think the interesting thing just to emphasize here is there already exists some type of recursion that people are used to in LLMs, which is chain of thought, but that is a recursion that's happening in the token space of the model's outputs, not inherent to the model itself. And that's sort of the fundamental limitation: the model can only do a feed-forward one-shot output. We basically just have this hack where if you keep letting it output things, then it can read its outputs and do somewhat intelligent-seeming things, but it seems to be upper-bounded by the data that we feed it that the labs are very hungrily buying right now and not this sort of inherent underlying recursive reasoning. Yeah, so in both cases, in both hacks, you're bounded by the bounds of human knowledge. In the event it's outside the set of human knowledge, then you're kind of SOL. And so that's one. The other, you make a great point about discrete versus latent space. President Trump announced a 25% tariff on European Union auto imports due to alleged trade deal violations and expressed skepticism toward a new Iranian proposal, citing "disjointed" leadership in Tehran.

Iran and Nuclear Security

  • Negotiation Status: President Trump confirmed that Iran is seeking a deal, but stated he is currently unsatisfied with their proposal.

      • Asset Loss: He stated their navy and air force have been "wiped out" and they currently lack functional radar or anti-aircraft capabilities.

      • Nuclear Proliferation: He criticized any nation that would allow Iran to possess a nuclear weapon, calling such a position "not very smart."

Trade and Auto Tariffs

  • EU Tariff Increase: The U.S. has raised tariffs on cars and trucks from the European Union to 25%.
      • Justification: Trump alleged the EU was not adhering to the existing trade agreement.
      • Production Incentives: The administration's goal is to force European manufacturers to move factory production to the United States.
  • Domestic Manufacturing Growth: Trump claimed that over $100 billion is currently being spent on building new car plants within the U.S.
      • Foreign Investment: He noted that companies from Japan, South Korea, Canada, and Mexico are among those currently expanding their U.S. manufacturing footprint.

Diplomatic Relations: Iraq and Pakistan

  • Iraq Leadership: Trump confirmed a phone call with the recent election winner in Iraq, whom he described as a "very good man."
      • U.S. Support: He claimed the victory occurred with "our help" and pledged that the United States is "with him all the way."
  • Pakistan Negotiations: The President expressed "tremendous respect" for the Prime Minister and the Field Marshal of Pakistan.
      • Remote Diplomacy: Due to the length of the trip, negotiations are currently being handled "telephonically" rather than by sending an envoy to Islamabad.

Executive Authority and Congress

  • Congressional Approval: When questioned about seeking congressional approval for certain extensions, Trump dismissed the necessity.
      • Constitutional Stance: He argued that such approval has never been sought by previous administrations and claimed that many consider it "totally unconstitutional" to require it.
      • Communication: He maintained that while he does not seek formal approval, the administration remains "in touch" with Congress.
